<p><b>Alpha Centauri</b> (<b>α Centauri</b>, abbreviated <b>Alpha Cen</b>, <b>α Cen</b>) is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars_and_brown_dwarfs" title="List of nearest stars and brown dwarfs">closest</a> <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star system</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> at a distance of 4.37 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (1.34 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">pc</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkinson2012_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson2012-5">[5]</a></sup> It consists of three stars: the <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">pair</a> <b>Alpha Centauri A</b> (also named <b>Rigil Kentaurus</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-CSN_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-CSN-12">[12]</a></sup>) and <b>Alpha Centauri B</b> together with a small and faint <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a>, <b>Alpha Centauri C</b> (also named <b><a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a></b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-CSN_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-CSN-12">[12]</a></sup>), that may be gravitationally bound to the other two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> To the unaided eye, the two main components appear as a single point of light with an apparent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_magnitude" title="Visual magnitude">visual magnitude</a> of −0.27, forming the brightest <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> in the southern <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_brightest_stars" title="List of brightest stars">third-brightest</a> star in the night sky, outshone only by <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>.</p>
<p>Alpha Centauri A (α Cen A) has 1.1 times the mass and 1.519 times the luminosity of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, while Alpha Centauri B (α Cen B) is smaller and cooler, at .907 times the Sun's mass and .445 times its visual luminosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> During the pair's 79.91-year orbit about a common centre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup> the distance between them varies from about that between <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> and the Sun to that between <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> and the Sun.</p>
<p>Proxima Centauri (α Cen C) is at the slightly smaller distance of 4.24 light-years (1.30 pc) from the Sun, making it the closest star to the Sun, even though it is not visible to the naked eye. The separation of Proxima from Alpha Centauri AB is about 15,000 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (0.24 ly; 0.07 pc),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> equivalent to 500 times the size of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>'s orbit. <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">Earth-sized</a> <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a> of Proxima Centauri, has been detected<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160824_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160824-17">[17]</a></sup> and may be a destination of future interstellar spacecraft, including a fleet of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/StarChip_(spacecraft)" title="StarChip (spacecraft)">StarChip</a></i> spacecraft currently being developed for a flyby mission by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breakthrough_Starshot" title="Breakthrough Starshot">Breakthrough Starshot</a> project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160824_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160824-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>α Centauri</i> (<a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">Latinised</a> to <i>Alpha Centauri</i>) is the system's <a href="/wiki/Bayer_designation" title="Bayer designation">Bayer designation</a>. It bore the traditional name <i>Rigil Kentaurus</i>, which is a latinisation of the Arabic name <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">رجل القنطورس</span> <i>Rijl al-Qanṭūris,</i> meaning "Foot of the <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaur</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K.26S_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-K.26S-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Alpha Centauri C</i> was discovered in 1915 by the <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> astronomer <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._A._Innes" title="Robert T. A. Innes">Robert Innes</a>, Director of the <a href="/wiki/Union_Observatory" title="Union Observatory">Union Observatory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Innes1915_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Innes1915-20">[20]</a></sup> who suggested that it be named <i>Proxima Centauri</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aj39_913_20_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-aj39_913_20-21">[21]</a></sup> (actually <i>Proxima Centaurus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Innes1917_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Innes1917-22">[22]</a></sup> The name is from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, meaning "nearest [star] of <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford2010_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford2010-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> organized a <a href="/wiki/IAU_Working_Group_on_Star_Names" title="IAU Working Group on Star Names">Working Group on Star Names</a> (WGSN)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGSN_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGSN-24">[24]</a></sup> to catalog and standardize proper names for stars. The WGSN states that in the case of <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">multiple stars</a> the name should be understood to be attributed to the brightest component by visual brightness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGSN2_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGSN2-25">[25]</a></sup> The WGSN approved the name <i>Proxima Centauri</i> for <i>Alpha Centauri C</i> on 21 August 2016 and the name <i>Rigil Kentaurus</i> for <i>Alpha Centauri A</i> on 6 November 2016. They are now both so entered in the IAU Catalog of Star Names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-CSN_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-CSN-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_and_components">Nature and components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Nature and components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">

<div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#Two_dimensions" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">billions of years</a>.<br/>

<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="164" data-file-width="452" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg/400px-Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg/600px-Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg/800px-Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The relative sizes and colours of stars in the Alpha Centauri system, compared to the Sun</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alpha Centauri is the name given to what appears as a single star to the naked eye and the <a href="/wiki/First_magnitude_star" title="First magnitude star">brightest</a> star in the southern constellation of <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a>. At −0.27 <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent visual magnitude</a> (<a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude#Magnitude_addition" title="Apparent magnitude">calculated</a> from A and B magnitudes), it is fainter only than <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>. The next-brightest star in the night sky is <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">Arcturus</a>. Alpha Centauri is a multiple-star system, with its two main stars being Alpha Centauri A <span class="nowrap">(α Cen A)</span> and Alpha Centauri B <span class="nowrap">(α Cen B)</span>, usually defined to identify them as the different components of the binary <span class="nowrap">α Cen AB</span>. A third companion—<a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a> (or Proxima or <span class="nowrap">α Cen C</span>)—has a distance much greater than the observed separation between stars A and B and is probably gravitationally associated with the AB system. As viewed from Earth, it is located at an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_separation" title="Angular separation">angular separation</a> of 2.2° from the two main stars. Proxima Centauri would appear to the naked eye as a separate star from <span class="nowrap">α Cen AB</span> if it were bright enough to be seen without a telescope. Alpha Centauri AB and Proxima Centauri form a <i>visual</i> <a href="/wiki/Double_star" title="Double star">double star</a>. Direct evidence that Proxima Centauri has an elliptical orbit typical of binary stars has yet to be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wds2006_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-wds2006-26">[26]</a></sup> Together, the three components make a triple <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star system</a>, referred to by double-star observers as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_star" title="Triple star">triple star</a> (or multiple star), <span class="nowrap">α Cen AB-C</span>.</p>
<p>Together, the bright visible components of the <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> system are called Alpha Centauri AB <span class="nowrap">(α Cen AB)</span>. This "AB" designation denotes the apparent gravitational centre of the main binary system relative to other companion star(s) in any multiple star system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-27">[27]</a></sup> "AB-C" refers to the orbit of Proxima around the central binary, being the distance between the centre of gravity and the outlying companion. Some older references use the confusing and now discontinued designation of A×B. Because the distance between the Sun and Alpha Centauri AB does not differ significantly from either star, gravitationally this binary system is considered as if it were one object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wds1996_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-wds1996-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">Asteroseismic</a> studies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromospheric_activity" title="Chromospheric activity">chromospheric activity</a>, and stellar rotation (<a href="/wiki/Gyrochronology" title="Gyrochronology">gyrochronology</a>), are all consistent with the α Cen system being similar in age to, or slightly older than, the Sun, with typical ages quoted between 4.5 and 7 billion years (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byr" title="Byr">Gyr</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mam08_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mam08-10">[10]</a></sup> Asteroseismic analyses that incorporate the tight observational constraints on the stellar parameters for α Cen A and/or B have yielded age estimates of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000484999999999999♠</span>4.85<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.5</span> Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thevenin02_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Thevenin02-7">[7]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000500000000000000♠</span>5.0<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.5</span> Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bazot12_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bazot12-29">[29]</a></sup> 5.2–7.1 Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miglio05_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miglio05-30">[30]</a></sup> 6.4 Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thoul03_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thoul03-31">[31]</a></sup> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000652000000000000♠</span>6.52<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.3</span> Gyr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggenberger04_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eggenberger04-32">[32]</a></sup> Age estimates for stars A and B based on chromospheric activity (Calcium H &amp; K emission) yield 4.4–6.5 Gyr, whereas gyrochronology yields <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000500000000000000♠</span>5.0<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.3</span> Gyr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mam08_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mam08-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_A">Alpha Centauri A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alpha Centauri A, also known as Rigil Kentaurus, is the principal member, or <i>primary</i>, of the binary system, being slightly larger and more luminous than the Sun. It is a solar-like <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> star with a similar yellowish colour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csiro_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-csiro-33">[33]</a></sup> whose <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">stellar classification</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_type" title="Spectral type">spectral type</a> G2 V. From the determined mutual orbital parameters, Alpha Centauri A is about 10 percent more massive than the Sun, with a radius about 23 percent larger. The <a href="/wiki/Stellar_rotation" title="Stellar rotation">projected rotational velocity</a> <span class="nowrap">( <i>v</i>·sin <i>i</i> )</span> of this star is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003270000000000000♠</span>2.7<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.7 km/s</span>, resulting in an estimated rotational period of 22 days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa470_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa470-34">[34]</a></sup> which gives it a slightly faster rotational period than the Sun's 25 days. When considered among the individual <a href="/wiki/List_of_brightest_stars" title="List of brightest stars">brightest stars</a> in the sky (excluding the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>), Alpha Centauri A is the fourth brightest at an apparent visual magnitude of +0.01, being fractionally fainter than Arcturus at an apparent visual magnitude of −0.04.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_B">Alpha Centauri B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alpha Centauri B is the companion star, or <i>secondary</i>, of the binary system, and is slightly smaller and less luminous than the Sun. It is a main-sequence star of spectral type K1 V, making it more an orange colour than the primary star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csiro_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-csiro-33">[33]</a></sup> Alpha Centauri B is about 90 percent the mass of the Sun and 14 percent smaller in radius. The projected rotational velocity <span class="nowrap">( <i>v</i>·sin <i>i</i> )</span> is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003110000000000000♠</span>1.1<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.8 km/s</span>, resulting in an estimated rotational period of 41 days. (An earlier, 1995 estimate gave a similar rotation period of 36.8 days.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau6259_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-iau6259-35">[35]</a></sup> Although it has a lower luminosity than component A, star B emits more energy in the <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> band. The <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> of B varies on a short time scale and there has been at least one observed flare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Alpha Centauri B at an apparent visual magnitude of 1.33 would be twenty-first in brightness if it could be seen independently of Alpha Centauri A.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_C_.28Proxima_Centauri.29">Alpha Centauri C (Proxima Centauri)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri C (Proxima Centauri)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alpha Centauri C, also known as <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>, is of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_class" title="Spectral class">spectral class</a> M6 Ve, a small main-sequence star (Type V) with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_lines" title="Emission lines">emission lines</a>. Its B−V <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_index" title="Colour index">colour index</a> is +1.82 and its mass is about 0.123 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></a>), or 129 <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass">Jupiter masses</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</div>
</div>
<p>The two Alpha Centauri AB binary stars are too close together to be resolved by the naked eye, because the angular separation varies between 2 and 22 arcsec,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> but through much of the orbit, both are easily resolved in binoculars or small 5 cm (2 in) telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the southern hemisphere, Alpha Centauri forms the outer star of <i>The Pointers</i> or <i>The Southern Pointers</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2-38">[38]</a></sup> so called because the line through <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">Beta Centauri</a> (Hadar/Agena),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonSA_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-NortonSA-39">[39]</a></sup> some 4.5<a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">°</a> west,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2-38">[38]</a></sup> points directly to the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a>—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Cross" title="Southern Cross">Southern Cross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2-38">[38]</a></sup> The Pointers easily distinguish the true Southern Cross from the fainter <a href="/wiki/Asterism_(astronomy)" title="Asterism (astronomy)">asterism</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Vela_(constellation)" title="Vela (constellation)">False Cross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="1280" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg/220px-Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg/330px-Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg/440px-Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The two bright stars at the lower right are Alpha (right) and <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">Beta Centauri</a> (left, above antenna). A line drawn through them points to the four bright stars of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Cross" title="Southern Cross">Southern Cross</a>, just to the right of the dome of <a href="/wiki/La_Silla_Observatory" title="La Silla Observatory">La Silla Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>South of about <a href="/wiki/29th_parallel_south" title="29th parallel south">29° S latitude</a>, Alpha Centauri is <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_star" title="Circumpolar star">circumpolar</a> and never sets below the horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Both stars, including Crux, are too far south to be visible for mid-latitude northern observers. Below about <a href="/wiki/29th_parallel_north" title="29th parallel north">29° N latitude</a> to the equator (roughly <a href="/wiki/Hermosillo" title="Hermosillo">Hermosillo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_City" title="Chihuahua City">Chihuahua City</a> in Mexico, <a href="/wiki/Galveston,_Texas" title="Galveston, Texas">Galveston, Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocala,_Florida" title="Ocala, Florida">Ocala, Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lanzarote" title="Lanzarote">Lanzarote</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> of Spain) during the northern summer, Alpha Centauri lies close to the southern horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonSA_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-NortonSA-39">[39]</a></sup> The star <a href="/wiki/Culmination" title="Culmination">culminates</a> each year at midnight on 24 April or 9 p.m. on 8 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonSA_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-NortonSA-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>As seen from Earth, Proxima Centauri is 2.2° southwest from Alpha Centauri AB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-44">[44]</a></sup> This is about four times the <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> of the Full Moon, and almost exactly half the distance between Alpha Centauri AB and Beta Centauri. Proxima usually appears as a deep-red star of an apparent visual magnitude of 13.1 in a sparsely populated star field, requiring moderately sized telescopes to see. Listed as V645 Cen in the <i><a href="/wiki/General_Catalogue_of_Variable_Stars" title="General Catalogue of Variable Stars">General Catalogue of Variable Stars (G.C.V.S.) Version 4.2</a></i>, this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_Ceti" title="UV Ceti">UV Ceti</a>-type <a href="/wiki/Flare_star" title="Flare star">flare star</a> can unexpectedly brighten rapidly by as much as 0.6 magnitudes at visual wavelengths, then fade after only a few minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bendict1998_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-bendict1998-45">[45]</a></sup> Some amateur and professional astronomers regularly monitor for outbursts using either optical or radio telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_history">Observational history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Observational history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="10695" data-file-width="10675" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg/170px-The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg/255px-The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg/340px-The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of Alpha Centauri from the <a href="/wiki/Digitized_Sky_Survey" title="Digitized Sky Survey">Digitized Sky Survey</a> 2</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>English explorer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hues" title="Robert Hues">Robert Hues</a> brought Alpha Centauri to the attention of European observers in his 1592 work <i>Tractatus de Globis</i>, along with <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Achernar" title="Achernar">Achernar</a>, noting "Now, therefore, there are but three Stars of the first magnitude that I could perceive in all those parts which are never seene here in England. The first of these is that bright Star in the sterne of Argo which they call <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canobus</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Achernar" title="Achernar">second</a> is in the end of <a href="/wiki/Eridanus_(constellation)" title="Eridanus (constellation)">Eridanus</a>. The third [Alpha Centauri] is in the right foote of the Centaure."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The binary nature of Alpha Centauri AB was first recognized in December 1689 by astronomer and Jesuit priest <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Richaud&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean Richaud (page does not exist)">Jean Richaud</a>. The finding was made incidentally while observing a passing <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> from his station in <a href="/wiki/Puducherry" title="Puducherry">Puducherry</a>. Alpha Centauri was only the second binary star system to be discovered, preceded by <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Crucis" title="Alpha Crucis">Alpha Crucis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KameswaraRao1984_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-KameswaraRao1984-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1752, French astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicolas_Louis_de_Lacaille" title="Nicolas Louis de Lacaille">Nicolas Louis de Lacaille</a> made <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrometric" title="Astrometric">astrometric</a> positional measurements using state-of-the-art instruments of that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Its large proper motion was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Manuel_John_Johnson" title="Manuel John Johnson">Manuel John Johnson</a>, observing from <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a>, who informed <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henderson_(astronomer)" title="Thomas Henderson (astronomer)">Thomas Henderson</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Royal Observatory, Cape of Good Hope">Royal Observatory, Cape of Good Hope</a> of it. The parallax of Alpha Centauri was subsequently determined by Henderson from many exacting positional observations of the AB system between April 1832 and May 1833. He withheld his results, however, because he suspected they were too large to be true, but eventually published them in 1839 after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Bessel" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel">Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel</a> released his own accurately determined parallax for <a href="/wiki/61_Cygni" title="61 Cygni">61 Cygni</a> in 1838.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated345_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated345-50">[50]</a></sup> For this reason, Alpha Centauri is sometimes considered as the second star to have its distance measured because Henderson's work was not fully recognized at first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated345_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated345-50">[50]</a></sup> (The distance of Alpha is now reckoned at 4.396 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">ly</a> or 41.59 trillion km.)</p>
<p>Later, <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> made the first <a href="/wiki/Micrometer" title="Micrometer">micrometrical</a> observations in 1834.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHerschel1847_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-JHerschel1847-51">[51]</a></sup> Since the early 20th century, measures have been made with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photographic_plates" title="Photographic plates">photographic plates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adsabs.harvard.edu_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-adsabs.harvard.edu-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="670" data-file-width="1026" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg/220px-Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg/330px-Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg/440px-Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Compared to the Sun, Alpha Centauri A is of the same stellar type G2, while Alpha Centauri B is a K1-type star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1926, South African astronomer <a href="/wiki/William_Stephen_Finsen" title="William Stephen Finsen">William Stephen Finsen</a> calculated the approximate <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbit elements</a> close to those now accepted for this system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> All future positions are now sufficiently accurate for visual observers to determine the relative places of the stars from a binary star <a href="/wiki/Ephemeris" title="Ephemeris">ephemeris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Others, like the Belgian astronomer D. Pourbaix (2002), have regularly refined the precision of any new published orbital elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alpha Centauri is inside the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G-cloud" title="G-cloud">G-cloud</a>, and the nearest known system to it is <a href="/wiki/Luhman_16" title="Luhman 16">Luhman 16</a> at 3.6 ly (1.1 pc).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-substellarcompanion_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-substellarcompanion-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scottish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._A._Innes" title="Robert T. A. Innes">Robert T. A. Innes</a> discovered Proxima Centauri in 1915 by blinking photographic plates taken at different times during a dedicated <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a> survey. This showed the large proper motion and parallax of the star was similar in both size and direction to those of Alpha Centauri AB, suggesting immediately it was part of the system and slightly closer to Earth than Alpha Centauri AB. Lying 4.24 ly (1.30 pc) away, Proxima Centauri is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">nearest star</a> to the Sun. All current derived distances for the three stars are from the <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallaxes</a> obtained from the <a href="/wiki/Hipparcos" title="Hipparcos">Hipparcos</a> star catalogue (HIP)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and the Hubble Space Telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_system">Binary system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Binary system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png/350px-Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png/525px-Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png/700px-Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Apparent and true orbits of Alpha Centauri. The A component is held stationary and the relative orbital motion of the B component is shown. The apparent orbit (thin ellipse) is the shape of the orbit as seen by an observer on Earth. The true orbit is the shape of the orbit viewed perpendicular to the plane of the orbital motion. According to the radial velocity vs. time <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pourbaix_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pourbaix-11">[11]</a></sup> the radial separation of A and B along the line of sight had reached a maximum in 2007 with B being behind A. The orbit is divided here into 80 points, each step refers to a timestep of approx. 0.99888 years or 364.84 days.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the orbital period of 79.91 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup> the A and B components of this binary star can approach each other to 11.2 AU (1.68 billion km), or about the mean distance between the Sun and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>; and may recede as far as 35.6 AU (5.33 billion km), approximately the distance from the Sun to <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken236_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken236-62">[62]</a></sup> This is a consequence of the binary's moderate <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> <i>e</i> = 0.5179.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup> From the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a>, the total mass of both stars is about 2.0 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>—or twice that of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken236_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken236-62">[62]</a></sup> The average individual stellar masses are 1.09 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> and 0.90 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>, respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stellar-model-kim_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-stellar-model-kim-64">[64]</a></sup> though slightly higher masses have been quoted in recent years, such as 1.14 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> and 0.92 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RECONS_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-RECONS-65">[65]</a></sup> or totalling 2.06 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>. Alpha Centauri A and B have <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitudes</a> of +4.38 and +5.71, respectively. <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">Stellar evolution</a> theory implies both stars are slightly older than the Sun at 5 to 6 billion years, as derived by both mass and their spectral characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stellar-model-kim_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-stellar-model-kim-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Viewed from Earth, the <i>apparent orbit</i> of this binary star means that its separation and <a href="/wiki/Position_angle" title="Position angle">position angle</a> (PA) are in continuous change throughout its projected orbit. Observed stellar positions in 2010 are separated by 6.74 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microarcsecond" title="Microarcsecond">arcsec</a> through the PA of 245.7°, reducing to 6.04 arcsec through 251.8° in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup> The closest recent approach was in February 2016, at 4.0 arcsec through 300°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The observed maximum separation of these stars is about 22 arcsec, while the minimum distance is 1.7 arcsec.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken.2C_R.G_1961.2C_p._235_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken.2C_R.G_1961.2C_p._235-67">[67]</a></sup> The widest separation occurred during February 1976 and the next will be in January 2056.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <i>true orbit</i>, closest approach or <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">periastron</a> was in August 1955, and next in May 2035. Furthest orbital separation at <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">apastron</a> last occurred in May 1995 and the next will be in 2075. The apparent distance between the two stars is rapidly decreasing, at least until 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proxima_Centauri">Proxima Centauri</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Proxima Centauri">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a></div>
<p>The much fainter <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a> Proxima Centauri, or simply Proxima, is about 15,000 astronomical units (AU) away from Alpha Centauri AB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adsabs.harvard.edu_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-adsabs.harvard.edu-52">[52]</a></sup> This is equivalent to 0.24 ly or 2.3 trillion km—about 5% the distance between Alpha Centauri AB and the Sun. Proxima is likely gravitationally bound to Alpha Centauri AB, orbiting it with a period between 100,000 and 500,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-44">[44]</a></sup> However, it is also possible that Proxima is not gravitationally bound and thus moving along a <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_trajectory" title="Hyperbolic trajectory">hyperbolic trajectory</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> with respect to Alpha Centauri AB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:72</sup> The main evidence for a bound orbit is that Proxima's association with Alpha Centauri AB is unlikely to be coincidental, because they share approximately the same motion through space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-44">[44]</a></sup> Theoretically, Proxima could leave the system after several million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup> It is not yet certain whether Proxima and Alpha Centauri are truly gravitationally bound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In a pre-print published 10 November 2016 P. Kervella and F. Thévenin showed that based on new high precision radial velocity measurements Proxima and Alpha Centauri are in fact gravitationally bound with a high degree of confidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The orbital period of Proxima is approximately 550,000 years, with an eccentricity of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999500000000000000♠</span>0.50<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.08</span>. Proxima comes within <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000430000000000000♠</span>4.3<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right">+1.1<br/>
−0.9</span></span></span> kAU of Alpha Centauri AB at periastron, and the apastron occurs at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001130000000000000♠</span>13.0<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right">+0.3<br/>
−0.1</span></span></span> kAU.</p>
<div style="overflow:auto;">
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:1002px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relative_positions_of_Sun,_Alpha_Centauri_AB_and_Proxima_Centauri.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="1000" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Relative_positions_of_Sun%2C_Alpha_Centauri_AB_and_Proxima_Centauri.png" width="1000"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relative_positions_of_Sun,_Alpha_Centauri_AB_and_Proxima_Centauri.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Relative positions of Sun, Alpha Centauri AB and Proxima Centauri. Grey dot is projection of Proxima Centauri, located at the same distance as Alpha Centauri AB.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Proxima is a red dwarf of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">spectral class</a> M6 Ve with an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of +15.60, which is only a small fraction of the Sun's luminosity. By mass, Proxima is calculated as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999123000000000000♠</span>0.123<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.06</span> <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> (rounded to 0.12 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>) or about one-eighth that of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa397_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa397-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinematics">Kinematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Kinematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png/350px-PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png/525px-PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png/700px-PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stars closest to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, including <i>Alpha Centauri</i> (25 April 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140425a_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140425a-73">[73]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All components of Alpha Centauri display significant <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motions</a> against the background sky, similar to the first-magnitude stars Sirius and Arcturus. Over the centuries, this causes the apparent stellar positions to slowly change. Such motions define the <i>high-proper-motion stars</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> These stellar motions were unknown to ancient astronomers. Most assumed that all stars were immortal and permanently fixed on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_Sphere" title="Celestial Sphere">celestial sphere</a>, as stated in the works of the philosopher Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> in 1718 found that some stars had significantly moved from their ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrometric" title="Astrometric">astrometric</a> positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> For example, the bright star <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">Arcturus</a> (α Boo) in the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Bo%C3%B6tes" title="Boötes">Boötes</a> showed an almost 0.5° difference in 1800 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> as did the brightest star, <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Canis_Major" title="Canis Major">Canis Major</a> (α CMa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Halley's positional comparison was <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s catalogue of stars contained in the <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> whose original data included portions from an earlier catalogue by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hipparchos" title="Hipparchos">Hipparchos</a> during the <span class="nowrap">1st century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Halley's proper motions were mostly for northern stars, so the southern star Alpha Centauri was not determined until the early 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken.2C_R.G_1961.2C_p._235_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken.2C_R.G_1961.2C_p._235-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scottish-born observer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henderson_(astronomer)" title="Thomas Henderson (astronomer)">Thomas Henderson</a> in the 1830s at the Royal Observatory at the Cape of Good Hope discovered the true distance to Alpha Centauri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson1839_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson1839-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> He soon realized this system displayed an unusually high proper motion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and therefore its observed true velocity through space should be much larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken.2C_R.G_1961.2C_p._235_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken.2C_R.G_1961.2C_p._235-67">[67]</a></sup> In this case, the apparent stellar motion was found using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicolas_Louis_de_Lacaille" title="Nicolas Louis de Lacaille">Nicolas Louis de Lacaille</a>'s astrometric observations of 1751–1752,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> by the observed differences between the two measured positions in different epochs. Using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hipparcos_Catalogue" title="Hipparcos Catalogue">Hipparcos Star Catalogue</a> (HIP) data, the mean individual proper motions are −3678 mas/yr or −3.678 arcsec per year in <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a> and +481.84 mas/yr or 0.48184 arcsec per year in <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HIP2008_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-HIP2008-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> As proper motions are cumulative, the motion of Alpha Centauri is about 6.1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcmin" title="Arcmin">arcmin</a> each century, and 61.3 arcmin or 1.02<a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">°</a> each <a href="/wiki/Millennium" title="Millennium">millennium</a>. These motions are about one-fifth and twice, respectively, the diameter of the full <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup> Using <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> the mean radial velocity has been determined to be around 20 km/s towards the Solar System.</p>
<p>As the stars of Alpha Centauri approach the Solar System, the measured proper motion and trigonometric parallax slowly increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HIP2008_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-HIP2008-88">[88]</a></sup> Changes are also observed in the size of the semi-major axis of the orbital <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>, increasing by 0.03 arcsec per century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-27">[27]</a></sup> This small effect is gradually decreasing until the star system is at its closest to Earth, and is then reversed as the distance increases again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-27">[27]</a></sup> Consequently, the observed position angles of the stars are subject to changes in the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a> over time, as first determined by W. H. van den Bos in 1926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Some slight differences of about 0.5 percent in the measured proper motions are caused by Alpha Centauri AB's orbital motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HIP2008_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-HIP2008-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="550" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg/260px-Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg/390px-Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg/520px-Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Apparent motion of Alpha Centauri relative to <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">Beta Centauri</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Based on these observed proper motions and radial velocities, Alpha Centauri will continue to gradually brighten, passing just north of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Cross" title="Southern Cross">Southern Cross</a> or <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a>, before moving northwest and up towards the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">celestial equator</a> and away from the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a>. By about 29,700 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AD" title="AD">AD</a>, in the present-day constellation of <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(constellation)" title="Hydra (constellation)">Hydra</a>, Alpha Centauri will be 3.26 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">ly</a> or 1.00 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">pc</a> away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup> Then it will reach the stationary radial velocity (RVel) of 0.0 km/s and the maximum apparent magnitude of −0.86v (which is comparable to present-day magnitude of <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>). Even during the time of this nearest approach, however, the apparent magnitude of Alpha Centauri will still not surpass that of Sirius, which will brighten incrementally over the next 60,000 years, and will continue to be the brightest star as seen from Earth for the next 210,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Alpha Centauri system will then begin to move away from the Solar System, showing a positive radial velocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-5"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup> Because of visual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perspective_(visual)" title="Perspective (visual)">perspective</a>, about 100,000 years from now, these stars will reach a final <a href="/wiki/Vanishing_point" title="Vanishing point">vanishing point</a> and slowly disappear among the countless stars of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>. Here this once bright yellow star will fall below naked-eye visibility somewhere in the faint present day southern constellation of <a href="/wiki/Telescopium" title="Telescopium">Telescopium</a>. This unusual location results from the fact that Alpha Centauri's orbit around the galactic centre is highly tilted with respect to the plane of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-6"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In about 4000 years, the <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a> of Alpha Centauri will mean that from the point of view of Earth it will appear close enough to Beta Centauri to form an optical double star. Beta Centauri is in reality far more distant than Alpha Centauri.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planets">Planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
