<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proxima_Centauri_b">Proxima Centauri b</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Proxima Centauri b">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a></div>
<p>In August 2016, the European Southern Observatory announced the discovery of a planet slightly larger than the Earth orbiting Proxima Centauri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proxima_b_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-proxima_b-94">[94]</a></sup> Proxima Centauri b was found using the radial velocity method, where periodic Doppler shifts of spectral lines of the host star suggest an orbiting object. From these readings, the radial velocity of the parent star relative to the Earth is varying with an amplitude of about 2 metres (6.6 ft) per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proxima_b_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-proxima_b-94">[94]</a></sup> The planet lies in the habitable zone of Proxima Centauri, but it is possible that the planet is tidally locked to the star,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proxima_b_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-proxima_b-94">[94]</a></sup> resulting in temperature extremes that would be difficult for life to overcome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tidallylocked_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-tidallylocked-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_Bb">Alpha Centauri Bb</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri Bb">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri_Bb" title="Alpha Centauri Bb">Alpha Centauri Bb</a></div>
<p>In 2012, a planet around Alpha Centauri B was announced, but in 2015 a new analysis concluded that it almost certainly does not exist and was just a spurious artefact of the data analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_Bc">Alpha Centauri Bc</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri Bc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 25 March 2015, a scientific paper by Demory and colleagues published transit results for Alpha Centauri B using the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> for a total of 40 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> They evidenced a transit event possibly corresponding to a planetary body with a radius around 0.92 <a href="/wiki/Earth_radius" title="Earth radius"><i>R</i><sub>⊕</sub></a>. This planet would most likely orbit Alpha Centauri B with an orbital period of 20.4 days or less, with only a 5 percent chance of it having a longer orbit. The median average of the likely orbits is 12.4 days with an impact parameter of around 0–0.3. Its orbit would likely have an eccentricity of 0.24 or less. If confirmed, this planet would be called Alpha Centauri Bc. Like the probably spurious Alpha Centauri Bb, it likely has lakes of molten lava and would be far too close to Alpha Centauri B to harbour life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_fourth_stellar_object">Possible fourth stellar object</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Possible fourth stellar object">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/U_(TNO)" title="U (TNO)">U (TNO)</a></div>
<p>In images taken on 7 July 2014 (343.5 GHz) and 2 May 2015 (445 GHz), researchers discovered a source in the <a href="/wiki/Far_infrared" title="Far infrared">far infrared</a> located within 5.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a> of <span class="nowrap">α Cen AB</span>. Based on its <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a>, it was at first thought to be a part of the Alpha Centauri system. Further analysis, however, found that the object must be closer to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, and that it may be gravitationally bound to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. The researchers suggest that the object may be an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extreme_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Extreme trans-Neptunian object">extreme trans-Neptunian object</a> (ETNO) beyond 100 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (15 billion kilometres), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">super-Earth</a> at around 300 AU (45 billion kilometres), or a very cool <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarf</a> at around 20,000 AU (3.0 trillion kilometres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liseau2015V1_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liseau2015V1-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes20151210_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbes20151210-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possibility_of_additional_planets">Possibility of additional planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Possibility of additional planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">planets orbiting other star systems</a>, including similar binary systems (<a href="/wiki/Gamma_Cephei" title="Gamma Cephei">Gamma Cephei</a>), raises the possibility that additional planets may exist in the Alpha Centauri system. Such planets could orbit Alpha Centauri A or Alpha Centauri B individually, or be on large orbits around the binary Alpha Centauri AB. Because both the principal stars are fairly similar to the Sun (for example, in age and <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>), astronomers have been especially interested in making detailed searches for planets in the Alpha Centauri system. Several established planet-hunting teams have used various <a href="/wiki/Radial_velocity" title="Radial velocity">radial velocity</a> or star <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">transit</a> methods in their searches around these two bright stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-universetoday.com_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-universetoday.com-103">[103]</a></sup> All the observational studies have so far failed to find any evidence for <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-universetoday.com_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-universetoday.com-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, computer simulations (then unaware of the close-in planet Bb) showed that a planet might have been able to form near the inner edge of Alpha Centauri B's habitable zone, which extends from 0.5 to 0.9 AU from the star. Certain special assumptions, such as considering that Alpha Centauri A and B may have initially formed with a wider separation and later moved closer to each other (as might be possible if they formed in a dense <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">star cluster</a>) would permit an accretion-friendly environment farther from the star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thebault_2009_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thebault_2009-105">[105]</a></sup> Bodies around A would be able to orbit at slightly farther distances due to A's stronger gravity. In addition, the lack of any brown dwarfs or gas giants in close orbits around A or B make the likelihood of terrestrial planets greater than otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lackofany_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-lackofany-106">[106]</a></sup> Theoretical studies on the detectability via radial velocity analysis have shown that a dedicated campaign of high-cadence observations with a 1-meter class telescope can reliably detect a hypothetical planet of 1.8 <a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass"><i>M</i><sub>⊕</sub></a> in the habitable zone of B within three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guedesetal2008_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-guedesetal2008-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Radial velocity measurements of Alpha Centauri B with <a href="/wiki/High_Accuracy_Radial_Velocity_Planet_Searcher" title="High Accuracy Radial Velocity Planet Searcher">High Accuracy Radial Velocity Planet Searcher</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectrograph" title="Spectrograph">spectrograph</a> ruled out planets of more than 4 <i>M</i><sub>⊕</sub> to the distance of the habitable zone of the star (orbital period P = 200 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumusque_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumusque-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Current estimates place the probability of finding an earth-like planet around Alpha Centauri A or B at roughly 85%, although this number remains uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miniature_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-miniature-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theoretical_planets">Theoretical planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Theoretical planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early computer-generated models of planetary formation predicted the existence of <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> around <a href="/wiki/Circumbinary_planet" title="Circumbinary planet">both Alpha Centauri A and B</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guedesetal2008_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-guedesetal2008-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> but most recent numerical investigations have shown that the gravitational pull of the companion star renders the accretion of planets very difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thebault_2009_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thebault_2009-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Despite these difficulties, given the similarities to the Sun in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_type" title="Spectral type">spectral types</a>, star type, age and probable stability of the orbits, it has been suggested that this stellar system could hold one of the best possibilities for harbouring <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> on a potential planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiegert_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wiegert-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lackofany_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-lackofany-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Solar System both Jupiter and Saturn were probably crucial in perturbing <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> into the inner Solar System. Here, the comets provided the inner planets with their own source of water and various other ices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-115">[115]</a></sup> In the Alpha Centauri system, Proxima Centauri may have influenced the planetary disk as the Alpha Centauri system was forming, enriching the area around Alpha Centauri A and B with volatile materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> This would be discounted if, for example, Alpha Centauri B happened to have gas giants orbiting Alpha Centauri A (or conversely, Alpha Centauri A for Alpha Centauri B), or if the stars B and A themselves were able to perturb comets into each other's inner system as Jupiter and Saturn presumably have done in the Solar System. Such icy bodies probably also reside in <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort clouds</a> of other planetary systems, when they are influenced gravitationally by either the gas giants or disruptions by passing nearby stars many of these icy bodies then travel starwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-7"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup> There is no direct evidence yet of the existence of such an Oort cloud around Alpha Centauri AB, and theoretically this may have been totally destroyed during the system's formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_69-8"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>To be in the star's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>, any suspected planet around Alpha Centauri A would have to be placed about 1.25 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> away <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span about="" title='"habitable'>citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> – about halfway between the distances of Earth's orbit and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>'s orbit in the Solar System – so as to have similar planetary temperatures and conditions for liquid water to exist. For the slightly less luminous and cooler Alpha Centauri B, the habitable zone would lie closer at about 0.7 AU (100 million km), approximately the distance that <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> is from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the goal of finding evidence of such planets, both Proxima Centauri and Alpha Centauri AB were among the listed "Tier 1" target stars for <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Space_Interferometry_Mission" title="Space Interferometry Mission">Space Interferometry Mission</a> (SIM). Detecting planets as small as three Earth-masses or smaller within two <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> of a "Tier 1" target would have been possible with this new instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-numbers_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-numbers-118">[118]</a></sup> The SIM mission, however, was cancelled due to financial issues in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="View_from_this_system">View from this system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: View from this system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="874" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg/350px-Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg/525px-Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg/700px-Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Looking towards the sky around Orion from Alpha Centauri with <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a> near <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">Betelgeuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Procyon" title="Procyon">Procyon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gemini_(constellation)" title="Gemini (constellation)">Gemini</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> between <a href="/wiki/Perseus" title="Perseus">Perseus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_(constellation)" title="Cassiopeia (constellation)">Cassiopeia</a> generated by <a href="/wiki/Celestia" title="Celestia">Celestia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Viewed from near the Alpha Centauri system, the sky would appear very much as it does for an observer on Earth, except that Centaurus would be missing its brightest star. The Sun would be a yellow star of an apparent visual magnitude of +0.5 in eastern <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_(constellation)" title="Cassiopeia (constellation)">Cassiopeia</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Antipodal_point" title="Antipodal point">antipodal point</a> of Alpha Centauri's current <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a> and <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a>, at 02<sup>h</sup> 39<sup>m</sup> 35<sup>s</sup> +60° 50′ (2000). This place is close to the 3.4-magnitude star <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Cassiopeiae" title="Epsilon Cassiopeiae">ε Cassiopeiae</a>. Because of the placement of the Sun, an interstellar or alien observer would find the \/\/ of Cassiopeia had become a /\/\/ shape<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[note 1]</a></sup> nearly in front of the <a href="/wiki/Heart_Nebula" title="Heart Nebula">Heart Nebula</a> in Cassiopeia. Sirius lies less than a degree from <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">Betelgeuse</a> in the otherwise unmodified <a href="/wiki/Orion_(constellation)" title="Orion (constellation)">Orion</a> and with a magnitude of −1.2 is a little fainter than from Earth but still the brightest star in the Alpha Centauri sky. <a href="/wiki/Procyon" title="Procyon">Procyon</a> is also displaced into the middle of <a href="/wiki/Gemini_(constellation)" title="Gemini (constellation)">Gemini</a>, outshining <a href="/wiki/Pollux_(star)" title="Pollux (star)">Pollux</a>, whereas both <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Vega</a> and <a href="/wiki/Altair" title="Altair">Altair</a> are shifted northwestward relative to <a href="/wiki/Deneb" title="Deneb">Deneb</a> (which barely moves, due to its great distance), giving the <a href="/wiki/Summer_Triangle" title="Summer Triangle">Summer Triangle</a> a more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equilateral" title="Equilateral">equilateral</a> appearance.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="View_from_Proxima_Centauri_b">View from Proxima Centauri b</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: View from Proxima Centauri b">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From Proxima Centauri b, Alpha Centauri AB would appear like two close bright stars with the combined apparent magnitude of −6.8. Depending on the binary's orbital position, the bright stars would appear noticeably divisible to the naked eye, or occasionally, but briefly, as a single unresolved star. Based on the calculated <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitudes</a>, the visual apparent magnitudes of Alpha Centauri A and B would be −6.5 and −5.2, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="View_from_a_hypothetical_A_or_B_planet">View from a hypothetical A or B planet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: View from a hypothetical A or B planet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Planet-alphacen1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Planet-alphacen1.png/260px-Planet-alphacen1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Planet-alphacen1.png/390px-Planet-alphacen1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Planet-alphacen1.png/520px-Planet-alphacen1.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Planet-alphacen1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's rendition of the view from a hypothetical airless planet orbiting Alpha Centauri A</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An observer on a hypothetical planet orbiting around either Alpha Centauri A or Alpha Centauri B would see the other star of the binary system as an intensely bright object in the night sky, showing a small but discernible disk while near periapse: A up to 210 arc seconds, B up to 155 arc seconds. Near apoapse, the disc would shrink to 60 arc seconds for A, 43 arc seconds for B, being too small to resolve by naked eye. In any case, the dazzling surface brightness could make the discs harder to resolve than a similarly sized less bright object.</p>
<p>For example, some theoretical planet orbiting about 1.25 AU from Alpha Centauri A (so that the star appears roughly as bright as the Sun viewed from the Earth) would see Alpha Centauri B orbit the entire sky once roughly every one year and three months (or 1.3(4) <a href="/wiki/Year" title="Year">a</a>), the planet's own <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a>. Added to this would be the changing apparent position of Alpha Centauri B during its long eighty-year elliptical orbit with respect to Alpha Centauri A (The average speed, at 4,5 degrees per Earth year, is comparable in speed to <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> here. With the eccentricity of the orbit, the maximum speed near periapse, about 18 degrees per Earth year, is faster than Saturn, but slower than Jupiter. The minimum speed near apoapse, about 1,8 degrees per Earth year, is slower than Neptune.). Depending on its and planet´s position on their respective orbits, Alpha Centauri B would vary in apparent magnitude between −18.2 (dimmest) and −21.0 (brightest). These visual apparent magnitudes are much dimmer than the <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of the Sun as viewed from the Earth (−26.7). The difference of 5.7 to 8.6 magnitudes means Alpha Centauri B would appear, on a linear scale, 2500 to 190 times dimmer than Alpha Centauri A (or the Sun viewed from the Earth), but also 190 to 2500 times brighter than the full <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> as seen from the Earth (−12.5).</p>
<p>Also, if another similar planet orbited at 0.71 AU from Alpha Centauri B (so that in turn Alpha Centauri B appeared as bright as the Sun seen from the Earth), this hypothetical planet would receive slightly more light from the more luminous Alpha Centauri A, which would shine 4.7 to 7.3 magnitudes dimmer than Alpha Centauri B (or the Sun seen from the Earth), ranging in apparent magnitude between −19.4 (dimmest) and −22.1 (brightest). Thus Alpha Centauri A would appear between 830 and 70 times dimmer than the Sun but some 580 to 6900 times brighter than the full Moon. During such planet's orbital period of 0.6(3) <a href="/wiki/Year" title="Year">a</a>, an observer on the planet would see this intensely bright companion star circle the sky just as humans see with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>'s planets. Furthermore, Alpha Centauri A's <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">sidereal period</a> of approximately eighty years means that this star would move through the local <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> as slowly as <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> with its eighty-four year period, but as the orbit of Alpha Centauri A is more elliptical, its apparent magnitude will be far more variable. Although intensely bright to the eye, the overall illumination would not significantly affect climate nor influence normal plant <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>An observer on the hypothetical planet would notice a change in orientation to <a href="/wiki/Very-long-baseline_interferometry" title="Very-long-baseline interferometry">very-long-baseline interferometry</a> reference points commensurate with the binary orbit periodicity plus or minus any local effects such as <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nutation" title="Nutation">nutation</a>.</p>
<p>Assuming this hypothetical planet had a low orbital inclination with respect to the mutual orbit of Alpha Centauri A and B, then the secondary star would start beside the primary at "stellar" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">conjunction</a>. Half the period later, at "stellar" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_(astronomy_and_astrology)" title="Opposition (astronomy and astrology)">opposition</a>, both stars would be opposite each other in the sky. As a net result, both the local sun and the other star would each be in sky for half a day, like sun and moon are both above horizon for half a day. But during stellar conjunction, the other star being "new" would be in sky in daytime, while during the opposition, the other star being "full" would be in sky for the whole night. In an Earth-like atmosphere, the light of the other star would be appreciably scattered, causing the sky to be perceptibly blue though darker than in daytime, like during twilight or total solar eclipse. Humans could easily walk around and clearly see the surrounding terrain, and reading a book would be quite possible without any artificial light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-115">[115]</a></sup> Over the following half period, the secondary star would be in sky for a progressively decreasing part of night (and increasing part of day) until at the next conjunction the secondary star would only be in sky in daytime near the primary star.</p>
<p>From a planet orbiting Alpha Centauri A or B, Proxima Centauri would appear as a fourth to fifth magnitude star, as bright as the faint stars of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Little_Dipper" title="Little Dipper">Little Dipper</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_names">Other names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Near-stars-past-future-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="999" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Near-stars-past-future-en.svg/350px-Near-stars-past-future-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Near-stars-past-future-en.svg/525px-Near-stars-past-future-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Near-stars-past-future-en.svg/700px-Near-stars-past-future-en.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Near-stars-past-future-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distances of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">nearest stars</a> from 20,000 years ago until 80,000 years in the future</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In modern literature, <i>Rigil Kent</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> (also <i>Rigel Kent</i> and variants;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[note 2]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/aɪ/ long 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/dʒ/ 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/əl/ 'le' in 'bottle'">əl</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɛ/ short 'e' in 'bed'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K.26S_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-K.26S-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup> and <i>Toliman</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup> were cited as colloquial alternative names of Alpha Centauri.</p>
<p><i>Rigil Kent</i> is short for <i>Rigil Kentaurus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup> which is sometimes further abbreviated to <i>Rigil</i> or <i>Rigel</i>, though that is ambiguous with <a href="/wiki/Rigel" title="Rigel">Beta Orionis</a>, which is also called Rigel. Although the short form <i>Rigel Kent</i> is often cited as an alternative name, the star system is most widely referred to by its <a href="/wiki/Bayer_designation" title="Bayer designation">Bayer designation</a> <i>Alpha Centauri.</i></p>
<p>The name <i>Toliman</i> originates with <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Golius" title="Jacobus Golius">Jacobus Golius</a>' edition of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Muhammad_ibn_Kathir_al-Farghani" title="Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Kathir al-Farghani">Al-Farghani</a>'s <i>Compendium</i> (published posthumously in 1669). <i>Tolimân</i> is Golius' latinization of the Arabic name <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">الظلمان <i>al-Ẓulmān</i></span> "the ostriches", the name of an asterism of which Alpha Centauri formed the main star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 19th century, the northern amateur popularist Elijah H. Burritt used the now-obscure name <i>Bungula</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> possibly coined from "β" and the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>ungula</i> ("hoof").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K.26S_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-K.26S-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Together, Alpha and Beta Centauri form the "Southern Pointers" or "The Pointers", as they point towards the Southern Cross, the <a href="/wiki/Asterism_(astronomy)" title="Asterism (astronomy)">asterism</a> of the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin_Chinese" title="Standard Mandarin Chinese">Standard Mandarin Chinese</a>, <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">南門</span> <i>Nán Mén</i>, meaning <i><a href="/wiki/Horn_(Chinese_constellation)" title="Horn (Chinese constellation)">Southern Gate</a></i>, refers to an asterism consisting of α Centauri and <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Centauri" title="Epsilon Centauri">ε Centauri</a>. Consequently, α Centauri itself is known as <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">南門二</span> <i>Nán Mén Èr</i>, the Second Star of the Southern Gate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>To the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_aboriginal" title="Australian aboriginal">Australian aboriginal</a> <a href="/wiki/Wergaia" title="Wergaia">Boorong people</a> of northwestern <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a>, Alpha and <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">Beta Centauri</a> are <i>Bermbermgle</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamacher_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-hamacher-132">[130]</a></sup> two brothers noted for their courage and destructiveness, who speared and killed <i>Tchingal</i> "The Emu" (the <a href="/wiki/Coalsack_Nebula" title="Coalsack Nebula">Coalsack Nebula</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanbridge_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanbridge-133">[131]</a></sup> The form in <a href="/wiki/Wergaia" title="Wergaia">Wotjobaluk</a> is <i>Bram-bram-bult</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamacher_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-hamacher-132">[130]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1280" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg/220px-The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg/330px-The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg/440px-The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Very_Large_Telescope_and_the_star_system_Alpha_Centauri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Very Large Telescope and star system Alpha Centauri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eso20170109_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-eso20170109-134">[132]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alpha Centauri is envisioned as a likely first target for manned or unmanned <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_travel" title="Interstellar travel">interstellar exploration</a>. Crossing the huge distance between the Sun and Alpha Centauri using current spacecraft technologies would take several millennia, though the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_pulse_propulsion" title="Nuclear pulse propulsion">nuclear pulse propulsion</a> or laser <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_sail" title="Light sail">light sail</a> technology, as considered in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breakthrough_Starshot" title="Breakthrough Starshot">Breakthrough Starshot</a> program, could reduce the journey time to a matter of decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160412-db_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160412-db-135">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Breakthrough Starshot is a proof-of-concept initiative to send a fleet of ultra-fast light-driven nanocraft to explore the Alpha Centauri system, which could pave the way for a first launch within the next generation. An objective of the mission would be to make a fly-by of, and possibly photograph, any planets that might exist in the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-starshot_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-starshot-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes20160412_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes20160412-139">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a>, announced by the <a href="/wiki/European_Southern_Observatory" title="European Southern Observatory">European Southern Observatory</a> (ESO) in August 2016, would be a target for the Starshot program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-starshot_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-starshot-138">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes20160824_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes20160824-140">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2017, Breakthrough Initiatives and the ESO entered a collaboration to enable and implement a search for habitable planets in the Alpha Centauri system.The agreement involves Breakthrough Initiatives providing funding for an upgrade to the <a class="external text" href="https://www.eso.org/public/teles-instr/vlt/vlt-instr/visir/" rel="nofollow">VISIR</a> (VLT Imager and Spectrometer for mid-Infrared) instrument on ESO's <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">Very Large Telescope</a> (VLT) in Chile. This upgrade will greatly increase the likelihood of planet detection in the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eso20170109_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-eso20170109-134">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronomy20170109_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronomy20170109-141">[139]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The coordinates of the Sun would be diametrically opposite Alpha Centauri AB, at <i>α</i>=02<sup>h</sup> 39<sup>m</sup> 36.4951<sup>s</sup>, <i>δ</i>=+60° 50′ 02.308″</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Spellings include <i>Rigjl Kentaurus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hyde" title="Thomas Hyde">Hyde T.</a>, "Ulugh Beighi Tabulae Stellarum Fixarum", <i>Tabulae Long. ac Lat. Stellarum Fixarum ex Observatione Ulugh Beighi</i>, Oxford, 1665, p. 142., Hyde T., "In Ulugh Beighi Tabulae Stellarum Fixarum Commentarii", <i>op. cit.</i>, p. 67., Portuguese <i>Riguel Kentaurus</i> da Silva Oliveira, R., <a class="external text" href="http://www.asterdomus.com.br/Artigo_crux_australis.htm" rel="nofollow">"Crux Australis: o Cruzeiro do Sul"</a>, Artigos: Planetario Movel Inflavel AsterDomus.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-157"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-157">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Weighted parallax based on parallaxes from van Altena <i>et al.</i> (1995) and Söderhjelm (1999).</span></li>
</ol>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.eso.org/public/images/potw1604a" rel="nofollow">"Our Nearest Star System Observed Live"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 January</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlpha+Centauri&amp;rft.btitle=Our+Nearest+Star+System+Observed+Live&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.eso.org%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fpotw1604a&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is calculated for a fixed latitude by knowing the star's <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> (δ) using the formulae (90°+ δ). Alpha Centauri's declination is −60° 50′, so the latitude where the star is circumpolar will be south of −29° 10′S or 29°. Similarly, the place where Alpha Centauri never rises for northern observers is north of the latitude (90°+ δ) N or +29°N.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-HIP2008-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-HIP2008_88-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HIP2008_88-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HIP2008_88-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.rssd.esa.int/index.php?project=HIPPARCOS&amp;page=hipsearch" rel="nofollow">European Space Agency: The Hipparcos and Tycho Catalogues Search facility(2008)</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Proper motions are expressed in smaller angular units than arcsec, being measured in milli-arcsec (mas.) or one-thousandth of an arcsec. A negative value for proper motion in RA indicates the sky motion is east to west, in declination north to south.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">van den Bos W. H. (1926). "Table of Visual Binary Stars". <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Union_Observatory_Circular&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Union Observatory Circular (page does not exist)">Union Observatory Circular</a></i>. <b>2</b>: 356.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlpha+Centauri&amp;rft.atitle=Table+of+Visual+Binary+Stars&amp;rft.au=van+den+Bos+W.+H.&amp;rft.date=1926&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Union+Observatory+Circular&amp;rft.pages=356&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=2"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Calculated as; <i>θ</i> − <i>θ</i><sub>o</sub> = <i>μ</i><sub>α</sub> × sin <i>α</i> × (<i>t</i> − <i>t</i><sub>o</sub> ), where; <i>α</i> = right ascension (in degrees), <i>μ</i><sub>α</sub> is the common <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a> (cpm.) expressed in degrees, and <i>θ</i> and <i>θ</i><sub>o</sub> are the current position angle and calculated position angle at the different epochs.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sky_and_Telescope" title="Sky and Telescope">Sky and Telescope</a></i>, April 1998 (p60), based on computations from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIPPARCOS" title="HIPPARCOS">HIPPARCOS</a> data.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Computed; using in solar terms: 1.1 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> and 0.92 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>, luminosities 1.57 and 0.51 <i>L</i>*/<i>L</i><sub>☉</sub>, Sun magnitude −26.73(v), 11.2 to 35.6 AU orbit; The minimum luminosity adds planet's orbital radius to A–B distance (max) (conjunction). Max. luminosity subtracts the planet's orbital radius to A–B distance (min) (opposition).</span></li>

Some known comets and spacecrafts, currently located within Solar System boundaries, are not gravitationally bound with the Sun, and therefore formally are separate (interstellar) objects.">rest</abbr> of <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a></small></li>
